1042</div>

1043<section id="_303">

1044<h3 id="_303_toc">

1045Incidence and Mortality</h3>

1046<p id="_552" tabindex="-1">

1047Estimated new cases and deaths from oropharyngeal cancer (included with oral cavity cancer) in the United States in 2023:[<a href="#cit/section_1.1">

10481</a>

1049]</p>

1050<div class="pdq-content-list">

1051<ul id="_553">

1052<li>

1053New cases: 54,540.</li>

1054<li>

1055Deaths: 11,580.</li>

1056</ul>

1057</div>

1058<p id="_305" tabindex="-1">

1059The increasing incidence of oropharyngeal cancer is attributed to the rise in human papillomavirus (HPV)-associated cases. Men are more than twice as likely as women to have oropharyngeal cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_1.1">

10601</a>

1061-<a href="#cit/section_1.3">

10623</a>

1063]</p>

1064</section>

1065<section id="_389">

1066<h3 id="_389_toc">

1067Anatomy</h3>

1068<p id="_390" tabindex="-1">

1069Anatomically, the oropharynx is located between the soft palate superiorly and the hyoid bone inferiorly. It is continuous with the oral cavity anteriorly and communicates with the nasopharynx superiorly and the supraglottic larynx and hypopharynx inferiorly. </p>

1070<p id="_391" tabindex="-1">

1071The oropharynx is divided into the following parts:[<a href="#cit/section_1.4">

10724</a>

1073]</p>

1074<div class="pdq-content-list">

1075<ul id="_293">

1076<li>

1077Base of the tongue, which includes the pharyngoepiglottic folds and the glossoepiglottic folds.</li>

1078<li>

1079Vallecula.</li>

1080<li>

1081 Tonsillar region, which includes the fossa and the anterior and posterior pillars.</li>

1082<li>

1083 Soft palate, which includes the uvula.</li>

1084<li>

1085 Posterior and lateral pharyngeal walls.</li>

1086</ul>

1087</div>

1088<section id="_392">

1089<h4 id="_392_toc">

1090Regional lymph node anatomy of the head and neck </h4>

1091<p id="_627" tabindex="-1">

1092The regional lymph nodes of the head and neck include the lymph nodes that run parallel to the jugular veins, spinal accessory nerve, and facial artery and into the submandibular triangle. An understanding of regional anatomy and the status of regional lymph nodes is critical to the care of patients with head and neck cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_1.3">

10933</a>

1094,<a href="#cit/section_1.5">

10955</a>

1096,<a href="#cit/section_1.6">

10976</a>

1098] To facilitate communication regarding lymph node anatomy, the regions of the neck are described as levels I to V and retropharyngeal:</p>

1099<div class="pdq-content-list">

1100<ul id="_628">

1101<li>

1102Level I contains the submental and submandibular lymph nodes.</li>

1103<li>

1104Level II contains the upper jugular lymph nodes, which are above the digastric muscle.</li>

1105<li>

1106Level III contains the midjugular lymph nodes, which are between the omohyoid muscle and the digastric muscle.</li>

1107<li>

1108Level IV contains the lower jugular lymph nodes. </li>

1109<li>

1110Level V contains the lymph nodes of the posterior triangle.</li>

1111<li>

1112Retropharyngeal lymph nodes.</li>

1113</ul>

1114</div>

1115<figure id="figure_623" class="image-center-medium">

1116<a href="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/779438.jpg" target="_blank" class="article-image-enlarge">

1117Enlarge</a>

1118<img id="_623" alt="Lymph node groups of the neck; drawing shows six groups of lymph nodes in the neck: group IA and IB, group IIA and IIB, group III, group IV, group VA and VB, and group VI." title="Lymph node groups of the neck; drawing shows six groups of lymph nodes in the neck: group IA and IB, group IIA and IIB, group III, group IV, group VA and VB, and group VI." src="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/779438-750.jpg">

1119</figure>

1120<p id="_395" tabindex="-1">

1121The retropharyngeal lymph nodes are a possible site for nodal spread in oropharyngeal cancer. A large retrospective cohort study from the MD Anderson Cancer Center described the clinical features of 981 patients with oropharyngeal cancer who underwent primary radiation therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_1.7">

11227</a>

1123][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

1124Level of evidence C1</a>

1125][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

1126Level of evidence C2</a>

1127] </p>

1128<div class="pdq-content-list">

1129<ul id="_396">

1130<li>

1131The base of the tongue (47%) and the tonsil (46%) were the most common primary sites. </li>

1132<li>

1133Most patients had stage T1 to T2 primary tumors (64%) and stage III to IVB disease (94%).</li>

1134<li>

1135The incidence of radiographic retropharyngeal node involvement was 10% and was highest for the pharyngeal wall (23%) and lowest for the base of the tongue (6%).</li>

1136<li>

1137Retropharyngeal lymph node involvement was associated with inferior 5-year local control and inferior recurrence-free survival, distant metastases&#8722;free survival, and overall survival on multivariate analysis.</li>

1138</ul>

1139</div>

1140</section>

1141</section>

1142<section id="_287">

1143<h3 id="_287_toc">

1144Risk Factors</h3>

1145<p id="_328" tabindex="-1">

1146Risk factors for oropharyngeal squamous cell carcinoma (SCC) include the following:[<a href="#cit/section_1.8">

11478</a>

1148]</p>

1149<div class="pdq-content-list">

1150<ul id="_289">

1151<li>

1152Smoking history of more than 10 pack years and other tobacco use.[<a href="#cit/section_1.9">

11539</a>

1154,<a href="#cit/section_1.10">

115510</a>

1156]</li>

1157<li>

1158Heavy alcohol use.</li>

1159<li>

1160<a href="/types/head-and-neck/hp/adult/oropharyngeal-treatment-pdq#_397">

1161HPV infection, especially HPV type 16, also known as HPV-16</a>

1162.[<a href="#cit/section_1.11">

116311</a>

1164-<a href="#cit/section_1.13">

116513</a>

1166]</li>

1167<li>

1168 <a href="/types/head-and-neck/hp/adult/oropharyngeal-treatment-pdq#_400">

1169Personal history of head and neck cancer</a>

1170.</li>

1171<li>

1172<a href="/types/head-and-neck/hp/adult/oropharyngeal-treatment-pdq#_535">

1173Betel quid chewing</a>

1174.</li>

1175</ul>

1176</div>

1177<p id="_527" tabindex="-1">

1178For more information, see <a href="/types/head-and-neck/hp/oral-prevention-pdq">

1179Oral Cavity, Oropharyngeal, Hypopharyngeal, and Laryngeal Cancers Prevention</a>

1180.</p>

1181<section id="_397">

1182<h4 id="_397_toc">

1183HPV infection</h4>

1184<p id="_398" tabindex="-1">

1185Because of the decreased incidence of smoking in the United States, HPV-negative, smoking-related oropharyngeal cancer is decreasing; however, HPV-positive oropharyngeal cancer is increasing. According to the Surveillance, Epidemiology, and End Results (SEER) Program's tissue repository data from 1988 to 2004, the prevalence of HPV-negative cancer declined by 50%, while HPV-positive oropharyngeal cancers increased by 225%.[<a href="#cit/section_1.14">

118614</a>

1187][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

1188Level of evidence C1</a>

1189]</p>

1190<p id="_399" tabindex="-1">

1191HPV-positive oropharyngeal cancers may represent a distinct disease entity that is caused by HPV infection and associated with an improved prognosis. Several studies indicate that individuals with HPV-positive tumors have significantly improved survival.[<a href="#cit/section_1.12">

119212</a>

1193,<a href="#cit/section_1.15">

119415</a>

1195-<a href="#cit/section_1.17">

119617</a>

1197] Due to the prognostic impact of the HPV status in oropharyngeal cancer, the American Joint Committee on Cancer 8th edition staging separates oropharyngeal staging by HPV status.[<a href="#cit/section_1.5">

11985</a>

1199,<a href="#cit/section_1.6">

12006</a>

1201] In a prospective study of 253 patients with newly diagnosed or recurrent head and neck SCC, HPV was detected in 25% of the patients. Poor tumor grade and an oropharyngeal site independently increased the probability of the presence of HPV.[<a href="#cit/section_1.12">

120212</a>

1203] Oropharyngeal tumors are more likely to be HPV positive (57%) than oral cavity (12%) tumor sites and non-oropharyngeal (14%) sites. HPV-positive oropharyngeal cancers predominantly arise in the palatine or lingual tonsils. For tonsil or base-of-tongue sites, 62% of tumors were HPV positive, compared with 25% for other oropharyngeal sites. </p>

1204</section>

1205<section id="_400">

1206<h4 id="_400_toc">

1207Personal history of head and neck cancer</h4>

1208<p id="_401" tabindex="-1">

1209The risk of developing a second primary tumor in patients with tumors of the upper aerodigestive tract has been estimated to be 3% to 7% per year.[<a href="#cit/section_1.18">

121018</a>

1211,<a href="#cit/section_1.19">

121219</a>

1213] Because of this risk, patients require lifelong surveillance. Smoking and alcohol consumption after treatment are associated with the development of second primary tumors of the aerodigestive tract.[<a href="#cit/section_1.20">

121420</a>

1215-<a href="#cit/section_1.22">

121622</a>

1217] Patients may need counseling to discontinue smoking and alcohol consumption. </p>

1218<p id="_402" tabindex="-1">

1219The process of field cancerization may be partly responsible for the multiple, synchronous, primary SCCs that occur in oropharyngeal cancer and that are associated with a smoking history. Originally described in 1953, the concept of field cancerization holds that tumors develop in a multifocal fashion within a field of tissue chronically exposed to carcinogens.[<a href="#cit/section_1.23">

122023</a>

1221] Molecular studies that detect genetic alterations in histologically normal tissue from high-risk individuals have provided strong support for this concept.[<a href="#cit/section_1.24">

122224</a>

1223-<a href="#cit/section_1.28">

122428</a>

1225]</p>

1226<p id="_403" tabindex="-1">

1227A comparison of patients (N = 2,230) with index SCC of the oropharynx site and index SCC of non-oropharyngeal sites (i.e., oral cavity, larynx, and hypopharynx) was performed to determine the likelihood of developing second primary malignancies. The second primary malignancy rate was lower for patients with index oropharyngeal SCC than for patients with index non-oropharyngeal cancer (<em>

1228P </em>

1229&lt; .001). Among patients with oropharyngeal SCC, former smokers had a 50% higher risk of second primary malignancy than never-smokers, and current smokers had a 100% higher risk than never-smokers (<em>

1230P</em>

1231 trend = .008). These data suggest that patients who fit the typical HPV phenotype have a very low risk of second primary malignancy.[<a href="#cit/section_1.29">

123229</a>

1233]</p>

1234</section>

1235<section id="_535">

1236<h4 id="_535_toc">

1237Betel quid</h4>

1238<p id="_536" tabindex="-1">

1239The chewing of betel quid, a stimulant preparation commonly used in parts of Asia, increases the risk of oropharyngeal cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_1.30">

124030</a>

1241]</p>

1242</section>

1243<section id="_404">

1244<h4 id="_404_toc">

1245Other risk factors</h4>

1246<p id="_405" tabindex="-1">

1247Other risk factors may include the following:[<a href="#cit/section_1.8">

12488</a>

1249]</p>

1250<div class="pdq-content-list">

1251<ul id="_406">

1252<li>

1253Defective elimination of acetaldehyde, a carcinogen generated by alcohol metabolism. In individuals, primarily those of East Asian race, who carry an inactive mutant allele of alcohol dehydrogenase-2, alcohol consumption is associated with a susceptibility to multiple metachronous oropharyngeal cancers that are caused by the decreased elimination of acetaldehyde.[<a href="#cit/section_1.31">

125431</a>

1255]</li>

1256</ul>

1257</div>

1258<p id="_407" tabindex="-1">

1259SCC of the oropharynx has not been associated with any specific chromosomal or genetic abnormalities. Genetic and chromosomal aberrations in these cancers are complex.[<a href="#cit/section_1.32">

126032</a>

1261,<a href="#cit/section_1.33">

126233</a>

1263] Despite the lack of specific genetic abnormalities, testing for genetic alterations or ploidy in early oropharyngeal lesions may identify patients who are at the greatest risk of disease progression and may lead to more-definitive therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_1.34">

126434</a>

1265]</p>

1266</section>

1267</section>

1268<section id="_408">

1269<h3 id="_408_toc">

1270Clinical Presentation </h3>

1271<p id="_409" tabindex="-1">

1272The clinical presentation of oropharyngeal cancer depends on the tumor&#8217;s location in the oropharynx. Oropharyngeal cancer may present in the following locations:</p>

1273<div class="pdq-content-list">

1274<ul id="_410">

1275<li>

1276<a href="/types/head-and-neck/hp/adult/oropharyngeal-treatment-pdq#_411">

1277Tonsil</a>

1278, tonsillar fossa, tonsillar pillars, or glossotonsillar sulci.</li>

1279<li>

1280<a href="/types/head-and-neck/hp/adult/oropharyngeal-treatment-pdq#_417">

1281Base of the tongue</a>

1282 (posterior one-third of the tongue posterior to the circumvallate papillae).</li>

1283<li>

1284Vallecula.</li>

1285<li>

1286<a href="/types/head-and-neck/hp/adult/oropharyngeal-treatment-pdq#_423">

1287Soft palate</a>

1288, inferior surface, or uvula.</li>

1289<li>

1290Posterior <a href="/types/head-and-neck/hp/adult/oropharyngeal-treatment-pdq#_425">

1291pharyngeal wall</a>

1292.</li>

1293</ul>

1294</div>

1295<section id="_411">

1296<h4 id="_411_toc">

1297Tonsil</h4>

1298<p id="_414" tabindex="-1">

1299The anterior tonsillar pillar and tonsil are the most common location for a primary tumor of the oropharynx.[<a href="#cit/section_1.4">

13004</a>

1301] Lesions involving the anterior tonsillar pillar may appear as areas of dysplasia, inflammation, or a superficial spreading lesion. These cancers can spread across a broad region, including the lateral soft palate, retromolar trigone and buccal mucosa, and tonsillar fossa.[<a href="#cit/section_1.3">

13023</a>

1303,<a href="#cit/section_1.4">

13044</a>

1305] The lymphatic drainage is primarily to level II nodes. </p>

1306<p id="_415" tabindex="-1">

1307Tumors of the posterior tonsillar pillar can extend inferiorly to involve the pharyngoepiglottic fold and the posterior aspect of the thyroid cartilage. These lesions more frequently involve level V nodes. </p>

1308<p id="_416" tabindex="-1">

1309Lesions of the tonsillar

1310fossa may be either exophytic or ulcerative and have a pattern of extension similar to those of the anterior tonsillar pillar. These tumors present as advanced-stage disease more often than do cancers of the tonsillar pillar. Approximately 75% of patients will present with stage III or stage IV disease.[<a href="#cit/section_1.3">

13113</a>

1312,<a href="#cit/section_1.4">

13134</a>

1314] The lymphatic drainage is primarily to level V nodes. Tumors of the posterior tonsillar pillar can extend inferiorly to involve the pharyngoepiglottic fold and the posterior aspect of the thyroid cartilage. These lesions more frequently involve level V nodes.</p>

1315<p id="_412" tabindex="-1">

1316Signs and symptoms of tonsillar lesions may include the following:[<a href="#cit/section_1.3">

13173</a>

1318,<a href="#cit/section_1.4">

13194</a>

1320]</p>

1321<div class="pdq-content-list">

1322<ul id="_413">

1323<li>

1324Pain.</li>

1325<li>

1326Dysphagia.</li>

1327<li>

1328Weight loss.</li>

1329<li>

1330Ipsilateral referred otalgia.</li>

1331<li>

1332A mass in the neck.</li>

1333</ul>

1334</div>

1335</section>

1336<section id="_417">

1337<h4 id="_417_toc">

1338Base of the tongue</h4>

1339<p id="_418" tabindex="-1">

1340Clinically, cancers of the base of the tongue are insidious. These cancers can grow in either an infiltrative or exophytic pattern. Because the base of the tongue is devoid of pain fibers, these tumors are often asymptomatic until there is significant tumor progression.[<a href="#cit/section_1.4">

13414</a>

1342]</p>

1343<p id="_419" tabindex="-1">

1344Signs and symptoms of advanced base-of-the-tongue cancers may include the following:[<a href="#cit/section_1.3">

13453</a>

1346,<a href="#cit/section_1.4">

13474</a>

1348]</p>

1349<div class="pdq-content-list">

1350<ul id="_420">

1351<li>

1352 Pain.</li>

1353<li>

1354Dysphagia.</li>

1355<li>

1356Weight loss.</li>

1357<li>

1358Referred otalgia secondary to cranial nerve involvement.</li>

1359<li>

1360Trismus secondary to pterygoid muscle involvement.</li>

1361<li>

1362Fixation of the tongue that is caused by infiltration of the deep muscle.</li>

1363<li>

1364A mass in the neck.</li>

1365</ul>

1366</div>

1367<p id="_422" tabindex="-1">

1368Lymph node metastasis is common because of the rich lymphatic drainage of the base of the tongue. Approximately 70% or more of patients with advanced base-of-the-tongue cancers have ipsilateral cervical nodal metastases; 30% or fewer of such patients have bilateral, cervical lymph&#8211;node metastases.[<a href="#cit/section_1.4">

13694</a>

1370,<a href="#cit/section_1.35">

137135</a>

1372] The cervical lymph nodes involved commonly include levels II, III, IV, and V and retropharyngeal lymph nodes. </p>

1373</section>

1374<section id="_423">

1375<h4 id="_423_toc">

1376Soft palate</h4>

1377<p id="_424" tabindex="-1">

1378Soft palate tumors are primarily found on the anterior surface.[<a href="#cit/section_1.4">

13794</a>

1380] Lesions in this area may remain superficial and in early stages.[<a href="#cit/section_1.3">

13813</a>

1382] The lymphatic drainage is primarily to level II nodes. </p>

1383</section>

1384<section id="_425">

1385<h4 id="_425_toc">

1386Pharyngeal wall</h4>

1387<p id="_426" tabindex="-1">

1388Pharyngeal wall lesions can spread superiorly to involve the nasopharynx, posteriorly to infiltrate the prevertebral fascia, and inferiorly to involve the pyriform sinuses and hypopharyngeal walls. Primary lymphatic drainage is to the retropharyngeal nodes and level II and III nodes. Because most pharyngeal tumors extend past the midline, bilateral cervical metastases are common. </p>

1389<p id="_427" tabindex="-1">

1390Early-stage tumors are often asymptomatic. Tumors of the pharyngeal wall are typically diagnosed in an advanced stage.[<a href="#cit/section_1.3">

13913</a>

1392,<a href="#cit/section_1.4">

13934</a>

1394]</p>

1395<p id="_428" tabindex="-1">

1396Signs and symptoms of advanced pharyngeal wall tumors may include the following:</p>

1397<div class="pdq-content-list">

1398<ul id="_429">

1399<li>

1400 Pain.</li>

1401<li>

1402Bleeding.</li>

1403<li>

1404Dysphagia.</li>

1405<li>

1406Weight loss.</li>

1407<li>

1408A mass in the neck.</li>

1409</ul>

1410</div>

1411</section>

1412<section id="_430">

1413<h4 id="_430_toc">

1414Leukoplakia</h4>

1415<p id="_431" tabindex="-1">

1416Leukoplakia is used only as a clinically descriptive term

1417meaning that the observer sees a white patch that does not rub off, the

1418significance of which depends on the histological findings.[<a href="#cit/section_1.8">

14198</a>

1420] Leukoplakia can range from

1421hyperkeratosis to an actual early invasive carcinoma or may represent a

1422fungal infection, lichen planus, or other benign oral disease.

1423</p>

1424</section>

1425</section>

1426<section id="_332">

1427<h3 id="_332_toc">

1428Diagnostic Evaluation</h3>

1429<p id="_432" tabindex="-1">

1430The assessment of the primary tumor is based on inspection and palpation, when possible, and by indirect mirror examination. The appropriate nodal drainage areas are examined by careful palpation. The presence of tumor must be confirmed histologically. Any other pathological data obtained from a biopsy and additional radiographical studies are also considered.</p>

1431<p id="_433" tabindex="-1">

1432The following procedures may be done to evaluate the primary tumor:</p>

1433<div class="pdq-content-list">

1434<ul id="_434">

1435<li>

1436Positron emission tomography&#8211;computed tomography (PET-CT) scan.</li>

1437<li>

1438Magnetic resonance imaging.</li>

1439<li>

1440Endoscopy.</li>

1441<li>

1442Laryngoscopy.</li>

1443<li>

1444Biopsy and p16 testing to assess for HPV status.</li>

1445</ul>

1446</div>

1447<p id="_435" tabindex="-1">

1448A PET-CT scan yields morphological and metabolic data to assess the detection of primary tumor, nodal disease, and distant metastatic disease. It may also be used to guide radiation therapy planning. Retrospective data demonstrate that morphological and PET-glycolytic parameters, which are measured in fluorodeoxyglucose PET-CT, are significantly larger (as measured by Response Evaluation Criteria In Solid Tumors [RECIST] longest diameter); more heterogenous; and have higher standardized uptake value (SUV) max, SUV mean, and metabolic tumor volume in HPV-negative disease compared with HPV-positive disease in the primary tumor for oropharyngeal carcinoma. However, the same PET parameters are frequently larger in the regional nodal disease in patients with HPV-positive disease.[<a href="#cit/section_1.36">

144936</a>

1450][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

1451Level of evidence C3</a>

1452]</p>

1453</section>

1454<section id="_438">

1455<h3 id="_438_toc">

1456Prognostic Factors and Survival</h3>

1457<p id="_439" tabindex="-1">

1458Prognostic factors for oropharyngeal carcinoma include the following:</p>

1459<div class="pdq-content-list">

1460<ul id="_440">

1461<li>

1462HPV status.</li>

1463<li>

1464Smoking history (pack-year smoking history of 10 or more years).</li>

1465<li>

1466Tumor stage and nodal status.</li>

1467</ul>

1468</div>

1469<p id="_441" tabindex="-1">

1470The criteria described in <a href="/types/head-and-neck/hp/adult/oropharyngeal-treatment-pdq#_528">

1471Table 1</a>

1472 are used to determine whether patients have low-, intermediate-, or high-risk oropharyngeal carcinoma. These criteria have been defined by using recursive partitioning analysis in a retrospective analysis of a randomized trial of patients with stage III and IV oropharyngeal SCC treated with chemoradiation.[<a href="#cit/section_1.17">

147317</a>

1474]</p>

1545</section>

1546<section id="_442">

1547<h3 id="_442_toc">

1548Follow-Up After Treatment</h3>

1549<p id="_443" tabindex="-1">

1550A careful examination of the patient's head and neck allows the physician to look for recurrence every 6 to 12 weeks for the first posttreatment year, every 3 months for the second year, every 3 to 4 months for the third year, and every 6 months thereafter.</p>

1551</section>

1696 <section id="_9">

1697

1698 <h2>

1699Cellular Classification of Oropharyngeal Cancer</h2>

1700

1701 <div id="_section_9" class="pdq-sections">

1702<p id="_238" tabindex="-1">

1703Most oropharyngeal cancers are squamous cell carcinomas (SCCs).[<a href="#cit/section_2.1">

17041</a>

1705-<a href="#cit/section_2.3">

17063</a>

1707] SCCs may be noninvasive or invasive. For noninvasive SCC, the term carcinoma <em>

1708in situ</em>

1709 is used. Histologically, invasive carcinomas are classified as well differentiated, moderately differentiated, poorly differentiated, or undifferentiated. SCCs are usually moderately or poorly differentiated.[<a href="#cit/section_2.4">

17104</a>

1711] Grading the deep invasive margins (i.e., invasive front) of SCC may provide better prognostic information than grading the entire tumor.[<a href="#cit/section_2.5">

17125</a>

1713] Human papillomavirus (HPV)-positive oropharyngeal cancers arising from the lingual and palatine tonsils are a distinct molecular-pathological entity that is linked to infection with HPV, especially HPV-16. Compared with HPV-negative tumors, HPV-positive tumors are more frequently poorly differentiated and nonkeratinizing. They are strongly associated with basaloid morphology and less likely to have <em class="gene-name">

1714TP53</em>

1715 mutations.[<a href="#cit/section_2.6">

17166</a>

1717] </p>

1718<p id="_444" tabindex="-1">

1719Immunohistochemical examination of tissues for the expression of the biomarker Ki-67, a proliferation antigen, may complement histological grading. As a molecular indicator of epithelial dysplasia of the oropharynx, Ki-67 expression appears to correlate well with loss of heterozygosity (LOH) in tumor cells. In a retrospective study involving 43 tissue samples from 25 patients, the assessment of proliferation with Ki-67 was a better surrogate for LOH than was histological grading.[<a href="#cit/section_2.7">

17207</a>

1721]</p>

1722<p id="_445" tabindex="-1">

1723Other types of oropharyngeal cancer include the following:</p>

1724<div class="pdq-content-list">

1725<ul id="_239">

1726<li>

1727Minor salivary gland tumors.</li>

1728<li>

1729Lymphomas.</li>

1730<li>

1731Lymphoepitheliomas (e.g., tonsillar fossa).</li>

1732</ul>

1733</div>

1734<p id="_240" tabindex="-1">

1735For more information, see <a href="/types/head-and-neck/hp/adult/salivary-gland-treatment-pdq">

1736Salivary Gland Cancer Treatment</a>

1737, <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/adult-hodgkin-treatment-pdq">

1738Hodgkin Lymphoma Treatment</a>

1739, <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/b-cell-lymphoma-treatment-pdq">

1740B-Cell Non-Hodgkin Lymphoma Treatment</a>

1741, and <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/peripheral-t-cell-lymphoma-pdq">

1742Peripheral T-Cell Non-Hodgkin Lymphoma Treatment</a>

1743.</p>

1774 <section id="_16">

1775

1776 <h2>

1777Stage Information for Oropharyngeal Cancer</h2>

1778

1779 <div id="_section_16" class="pdq-sections">

1780<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

1781<h6>

1782In This Section</h6>

1783<ul>

1784<li>

1785<a href="#_273_toc">

1786American Joint Committee on Cancer (AJCC) Staging Groupings and TNM Definitions<ul>

1787<li>

1788<a href="#_700_toc">

1789AJCC prognostic stage groups for HPV-mediated (p16-positive) oropharyngeal cancer</a>

1790</li>

1791<li>

1792<a href="#_705_toc">

1793AJCC prognostic stage groups for p16-negative squamous cancers of the oropharynx</a>

1794</li>

1795</ul>

1796</a>

1797</li>

1798</ul>

1799</nav>

1800<p id="_17" tabindex="-1">

1801The staging system for oropharyngeal cancer is clinical rather than pathological. It is based on the best

1802estimate of the extent of disease before treatment.</p>

1803<p id="_446" tabindex="-1">

1804Clinical anatomical staging of oropharyngeal cancer involves the following clinical assessment and imaging techniques:</p>

1805<div class="pdq-content-list">

1806<ul id="_447">

1807<li>

1808Inspection and palpation of sites (when feasible) and neck nodes.</li>

1809<li>

1810 Neurological examination of all cranial nerves. </li>

1811<li>

1812A head and neck computed tomography (CT) scan with contrast to evaluate the mandible and maxilla.[<a href="#cit/section_3.1">

18131</a>

1814]</li>

1815<li>

1816Magnetic resonance imaging to evaluate the extent of disease in the soft tissues.</li>

1817<li>

1818Positron emission tomography (PET)&#8211;CT scan to assess primary, regional, and distant metastatic spread.</li>

1819<li>

1820Complete endoscopy is performed after completion of other staging studies to assess the surface extent of the tumor accurately and to facilitate biopsy. This procedure is typically performed under general anesthesia, which also allows palpation for deep muscle invasion. Because of the incidence of multiple primary tumors occurring simultaneously, a careful search for other primary tumors of the upper aerodigestive tract is indicated.[<a href="#cit/section_3.2">

18212</a>

1822]</li>

1823</ul>

1824</div>

1825<p id="_448" tabindex="-1">

1826PET has been investigated as an imaging modality for recurrent oropharyngeal cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_3.3">

18273</a>

1828]</p>

1829<section id="_273">

1830<h3 id="_273_toc">

1831American Joint Committee on Cancer (AJCC) Staging Groupings and TNM Definitions</h3>

1832<p id="_259" tabindex="-1">

1833The AJCC has designated staging by TNM

1834(tumor, node, metastasis) classification to define oropharyngeal cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_3.2">

18352</a>

1836,<a href="#cit/section_3.4">

18374</a>

1838] Nonepithelial tumors such as those of lymphoid tissue, soft tissue, bone, and cartilage are not included.

1839</p>

1840<p id="_699" tabindex="-1">

1841The AJCC uses separate staging systems for human papillomavirus (HPV)-related squamous cell carcinoma of the oropharynx [<a href="#cit/section_3.4">

18424</a>

1843] and p16-negative squamous cancers of the oropharynx.[<a href="#cit/section_3.2">

18442</a>

1845]</p>

1846<section id="_700">

1847<h4 id="_700_toc">

1848AJCC prognostic stage groups for HPV-mediated (p16-positive) oropharyngeal cancer</h4>

2554&#8722;T4b = Very advanced local disease. Tumor invades lateral pterygoid muscle, pterygoid plates, lateral nasopharynx, or skull base or encases carotid artery.</td>

2555</tr>

2556<tr>

2557<td>

2558NX = Regional lymph nodes cannot be assessed.</td>

2559</tr>

2560<tr>

2561<td>

2562N0 = No regional lymph node metastasis.</td>

2563</tr>

2564<tr>

2565<td>

2566N1 = Metastasis in a single ipsilateral lymph node, &#8804;3 cm in greatest dimension and ENE(&#8722;).</td>

2567</tr>

2568<tr>

2569<td>

2570N2 = Metastasis in a single ipsilateral node &gt;3 cm but &#8804;6 cm in greatest dimension and ENE(&#8722;); <em>

2571or</em>

2572 metastases in multiple ipsilateral lymph nodes, none &gt;6 cm in greatest dimension and ENE(&#8722;); <em>

2573or</em>

2574 in bilateral or contralateral lymph nodes, none &gt;6 cm in greatest dimension and ENE(&#8722;).</td>

2575</tr>

2576<tr>

2577<td>

2578&#8722;N2a = Metastasis in a single ipsilateral node &gt;3 cm but &#8804;6 cm in greatest dimension and ENE(&#8722;).</td>

2579</tr>

2580<tr>

2581<td>

2582&#8722;N2b = Metastases in multiple ipsilateral nodes, none &gt;6 cm in greatest dimension and ENE(&#8722;).</td>

2583</tr>

2584<tr>

2585<td>

2586&#8722;N2c = Metastases in bilateral or contralateral lymph nodes, none &gt;6 cm in greatest dimension and ENE(&#8722;).</td>

2587</tr>

2588<tr>

2589<td>

2590N3 = Metastasis in a lymph node &gt;6 cm in greatest dimension and ENE(&#8722;); <em>

2591or</em>

2592 metastasis in any node(s) and clinically overt ENE(+).</td>

2593</tr>

2594<tr>

2595<td>

2596&#8722;N3a = Metastasis in a lymph node &gt;6 cm in greatest dimension and ENE(&#8722;).</td>

2597</tr>

2598<tr>

2599<td>

2600&#8722;N3b = Metastasis in any node(s) and clinically overt ENE(+).</td>

2601</tr>

2602<tr>

2603<td>

2604M1 = Distant metastasis.</td>

2605</tr>

2606</tbody>

2607</table>

2632 <section id="_49">

2633

2634 <h2>

2635Treatment Option Overview for Oropharyngeal Cancer</h2>

2636

2637 <div id="_section_49" class="pdq-sections">

2638<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

2639<h6>

2640In This Section</h6>

2641<ul>

2642<li>

2643<a href="#_334_toc">

2644Surgery and/or Radiation Therapy</a>

2645</li>

2646<li>

2647<a href="#_554_toc">

2648Surgery Followed by Postoperative Radiation Therapy (PORT) With or Without Concurrent Chemotherapy for Patients With Locally Advanced Disease</a>

2649</li>

2650<li>

2651<a href="#_337_toc">

2652Radiation Therapy<ul>

2653<li>

2654<a href="#_597_toc">

2655Altered fractionation versus standard fractionation radiation therapy</a>

2656</li>

2657</ul>

2658</a>

2659</li>

2660<li>

2666</li>

2667</ul>

2668</a>

2669</li>

2670<li>

2672Neoadjuvant Chemotherapy Followed by Concurrent Chemoradiation Therapy</a>

2673</li>

2674</ul>

2675</nav>

2676<p id="_51" tabindex="-1">

2677An optimal approach for the treatment of oropharyngeal cancer is not easily defined because no single regimen offers a clear-cut, superior-survival advantage. The literature reports

2678 various therapeutic options but does not present any valid comparative studies of these options. Treatment considerations should account for functional and performance status, including speech and swallowing outcomes.</p>

2805<section id="_334">

2806<h3 id="_334_toc">

2807Surgery and/or Radiation Therapy</h3>

2808<p id="_381" tabindex="-1">

2809Surgery and radiation therapy have been the standard treatment for oropharyngeal cancer; however, outcome data from randomized trials are limited. Studies have evaluated whether to use surgery or radiation but have been underpowered.[<a href="#cit/section_4.1">

28101</a>

2811]</p>

2812<p id="_450" tabindex="-1">

2813Evidence (surgery and/or radiation therapy):</p>

2814<div class="pdq-content-list">

2815<ol id="_451">

2816<li>

2817In a prospective randomized trial, 564 patients with head and neck cancer and N2 or N3 disease were assigned to either planned neck dissection or surveillance with positron emission tomography&#8211;computed tomography (PET-CT) scan.[<a href="#cit/section_4.2">

28182</a>

2819]<div class="pdq-content-list">

2820<ul id="_626">

2821<li>

2822With a median follow up of 36 months, PET-CT surveillance resulted in fewer neck dissections compared with the surgical group (54 vs. 221), with a 2-year survival rate of 84.9% for the neck dissection group and 81.5% for the surgical group. The hazard ratio (HR) for death (HR<sub>

2823death</sub>

2824) slightly favored PET-CT&#8722;guided surveillance and indicated noninferiority (upper boundary 95% confidence interval [CI] for HR &lt;1.50; <em>

2825P</em>

2826 = .004).</li>

2827</ul>

2828</div>

2829</li>

2830<li>

2831A pooled analysis of 6,400 patients with base-of-the-tongue oropharyngeal carcinoma from 51 reported series between 1970 and 2000 demonstrated the following:[<a href="#cit/section_4.3">

28323</a>

2833]<div class="pdq-content-list">

2834<ul id="_452">

2835<li>

2836Local control rates of 79% (surgery with or without radiation therapy) and 76% (radiation therapy alone), (<em>

2837P</em>

2838 = .087); locoregional control was 60% for surgery with or without radiation therapy versus 69% for radiation therapy alone (<em>

2839P</em>

2840 = .009).</li>

2841<li>

2842The 5-year survival rate was 49% for surgery with or without radiation therapy versus 52% (<em>

2843P</em>

2844 = .2) for radiation therapy with or without neck dissection.</li>

2845<li>

2846The rate of severe complications was 32% for the surgery group versus 3.8% for the radiation therapy group (<em>

2847P</em>

2848 &lt; .001).</li>

2849<li>

2850The rate of fatal complications was 3.5% for the surgery group versus 0.4% for the radiation therapy group (<em>

2851P</em>

2852 &lt; .001).</li>

2853</ul>

2854</div>

2855<p id="_453" tabindex="-1">

2856Historically, the posttreatment performance and functional status of patients with base-of-the-tongue primary tumors was better after radiation therapy than

2857after surgery. Local control and survival are similar in both treatment options.[<a href="#cit/section_4.4">

28584</a>

2859,<a href="#cit/section_4.5">

28605</a>

2861]</p>

2862</li>

2863<li>

2864 In the same study, the results for patients with squamous cell carcinoma (SCC) in the tonsillar region who underwent surgery with or without radiation therapy versus radiation therapy with or without neck dissection were as follows:[<a href="#cit/section_4.3">

28653</a>

2866]<div class="pdq-content-list">

2867<ul id="_454">

2868<li>

2869Local control rates of 70% (surgery with or without radiation therapy) and 68% (radiation therapy), (<em>

2870P</em>

2871 = .2); locoregional control was 65% for surgery with or without radiation therapy versus 69% for radiation therapy alone (<em>

2872P</em>

2873 = .1).</li>

2874<li>

2875The 5-year survival rate was 47% for surgery with or without radiation therapy versus 43% (<em>

2876P</em>

2877 = .2) for radiation therapy with or without neck dissection.</li>

2878<li>

2879The rate of severe complications was 23% for the surgery group versus 6% for the radiation therapy group (<em>

2880P</em>

2881 &lt; .001)</li>

2882<li>

2883 The rate of fatal complications was 3.2% for the surgery group versus 0.8% for the radiation therapy group (<em>

2884P</em>

2885 &lt; .001).</li>

2886</ul>

2887</div>

2888</li>

2889</ol>

2890</div>

2891<p id="_382" tabindex="-1">

2892For patients with early-stage disease, single-modality treatment is preferred. Historically, radiation alone has been preferred, although use of new surgical techniques, including transoral surgery and transoral robotic surgery, is increasing. Nonrandomized comparisons of transoral surgery versus primary radiation therapy suggest superior quality of life (QOL) with minimally invasive surgical techniques.[<a href="#cit/section_4.6">

28936</a>

2894] Historically, more&#8211;invasive surgical techniques were associated with inferior QOL and greater morbidity.</p>

2895<p id="_336" tabindex="-1">

2896A prospective multicenter trial (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT01898494">

2897ECOG-3311</a>

2898 [NCT01898494]) evaluating transoral surgery approaches in human papillomavirus&#8722;positive oropharyngeal carcinoma with postoperative radiation dose de-escalation is currently under way.</p>

2899</section>

2900<section id="_554">

2901<h3 id="_554_toc">

2902Surgery Followed by Postoperative Radiation Therapy (PORT) With or Without Concurrent Chemotherapy for Patients With Locally Advanced Disease</h3>

2903<p id="_584" tabindex="-1">

2904New

2905surgical techniques for resection and reconstruction that provide access and functional preservation have

2906extended the surgical options for patients with stage III or stage IV oropharyngeal cancer. Specific surgical procedures and their modifications

2907are not described here because of the wide variety of

2908surgical approaches, the variety of opinions about the role of modified neck

2909dissections, and the multiple reconstructive

2910techniques that may give the same results. This group of patients are

2911managed by head and neck surgeons who are skilled in the multiple procedures available and are actively and frequently involved in the care of these patients.</p>

2912<p id="_555" tabindex="-1">

2913Depending on pathological findings after primary surgery, PORT with or without chemotherapy is used in the adjuvant setting for patients with the following histological findings:</p>

2914<div class="pdq-content-list">

2915<ul id="_556">

2916<li>

2917 T4 disease.</li>

2918<li>

2919Perineural invasion.</li>

2920<li>

2921Lymphovascular invasion.</li>

2922<li>

2923Positive margins or margins less than 5 mm.</li>

2924<li>

2925Extracapsular extension of a lymph node.</li>

2926<li>

2927Two or more involved lymph nodes.</li>

2928</ul>

2929</div>

2930<p id="_557" tabindex="-1">

2931The addition of chemotherapy to PORT for oropharyngeal SCC demonstrates a locoregional control and overall survival (OS) benefit compared with radiation therapy alone in patients who have high-risk pathological risk factors, extracapsular extension (ECE) of a lymph node, or positive margins, based on a pooled analysis of the <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00002555">

2932EORTC-22931</a>

2933 (NCT00002555) and <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00002670">

2934RTOG-9501</a>

2935 (NCT00002670) studies.[<a href="#cit/section_4.7">

29367</a>

2937-<a href="#cit/section_4.10">

293810</a>

2939][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2940Level of evidence A1</a>

2941] </p>

2942<p id="_558" tabindex="-1">

2943For patients with intermediate pathological risk factors, the addition of cisplatin chemotherapy given concurrently with PORT is unclear. Intermediate pathological risk factors include the following:</p>

2944<div class="pdq-content-list">

2945<ul id="_559">

2946<li>

2947T3 and T4 disease (or stage III and stage IV disease). </li>

2948<li>

2949Perineural infiltration. </li>

2950<li>

2951Vascular embolisms. </li>

2952<li>

2953Clinically enlarged level IV&#8211;V lymph nodes secondary to tumors arising in the oral cavity or oropharynx. </li>

2954<li>

2955Two or more histopathologically involved lymph nodes without ECE. </li>

2956<li>

2957Close margins less than 5 mm. </li>

2958</ul>

2959</div>

2960<p id="_560" tabindex="-1">

2961The addition of cetuximab with radiation therapy in the postoperative setting for these intermediate pathological risk factors is being tested in a randomized trial (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00956007">

2962RTOG-0920</a>

2963 [NCT00956007]). </p>

2964</section>

2965<section id="_337">

2966<h3 id="_337_toc">

2967Radiation Therapy</h3>

2968<p id="_586" tabindex="-1">

2969A review of published clinical results of radiation therapy for head

2970and neck cancer suggested a significant loss of local control when the

2971administration of radiation therapy was prolonged. Therefore, extending

2972standard treatment schedules is detrimental.[<a href="#cit/section_4.11">

297311</a>

2974,<a href="#cit/section_4.12">

297512</a>

2976]</p>

2977<p id="_587" tabindex="-1">

2978Patients who are smokers appear to have lower

2979response rates and shorter survival times than those who do not smoke while receiving radiation therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_4.13">

298013</a>

2981]

2982Counseling patients to stop smoking before beginning

2983radiation therapy may be beneficial. </p>

2984<p id="_588" tabindex="-1">

2985Intensity-modulated radiation therapy (IMRT) has become a standard technique for head and neck radiation therapy. IMRT allows a dose-painting technique, also known as a simultaneous-integrated-boost (SIB) technique, with a dose per fraction slightly higher than 2 Gy, which allows slight shortening of overall treatment time and increases the biologically equivalent dose to the tumor.</p>

2986<p id="_589" tabindex="-1">

2987Evidence (definitive radiation therapy):</p>

2988<div class="pdq-content-list">

2989<ol id="_590">

2990<li>

2991IMRT was studied in a phase II trial (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00006360">

2992RTOG-0022</a>

2993 [NCT00006360]) of 69 patients with stages T1 to T2, N0 to N1, M0 oropharyngeal carcinoma who were treated with primary radiation therapy without chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_4.14">

299414</a>

2995] The median follow-up was 2.8 years. The prescribed planning target volume (PTV) dose to the primary tumor and involved nodes was 66 Gy at 2.2 Gy per fraction over 6 weeks. Subclinical PTVs received simultaneously 54 to 60 Gy at 1.8 to 2.0 Gy per fraction using an SIB technique. The following results were observed:<div class="pdq-content-list">

2996<ul id="_591">

2997<li>

2998The 2-year estimated locoregional failure rate was 9%. Two of four patients (50%), who had major underdose deviations, had locoregional failure compared with 3 of 49 patients (6%) without such deviations (<em>

2999P</em>

3000 = .04).</li>

3001<li>

3002Maximal late toxicities with a grade of 2 or higher were skin (12%), mucosa (24%), salivary (67%), esophagus (19%), and osteoradionecrosis (6%).</li>

3003<li>

3004Longer follow-up revealed reduced late toxicity in all categories. Xerostomia grade 2 or higher was observed in 55% of patients at 6 months but was reduced to 25% of patients at 12 months and 16% of patients at 24 months. </li>

3005</ul>

3006</div>

3007<p id="_592" tabindex="-1">

3008The <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00006360">

3009RTOG-0022</a>

3010 study showed high control rates and the feasibility of IMRT at a multi-institutional level; the study also showed high tumor control rates and reduced salivary toxicity compared with previous Radiation Therapy Oncology Group (RTOG) studies. However, major target underdose deviations were associated with a higher locoregional failure rate.</p>

3011</li>

3012<li>

3013Similar nonrandomized multicenter studies used fractionally escalated doses, ranging from 2.3 to 2.5 Gy with IMRT. These doses have been safe when given without concurrent chemotherapy for pharyngolaryngeal T2N0, T2N1, or laryngeal T3N0 SCC.[<a href="#cit/section_4.15">

301415</a>

3015-<a href="#cit/section_4.19">

301619</a>

3017]<div class="pdq-content-list">

3018<ul id="_593">

3019<li>

3020No toxicity difference was observed between the different dose-escalated groups.</li>

3021</ul>

3022</div>

3023</li>

3024<li>

3025A randomized trial (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00081029">

3026PARSPORT</a>

3027 [NCT00081029]) conducted in the United Kingdom compared conventional 3-dimensional conformal radiation therapy with IMRT. The following results were observed:[<a href="#cit/section_4.20">

302820</a>

3029][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3030Level of evidence A1</a>

3031]<div class="pdq-content-list">

3032<ul id="_594">

3033<li>

3034The rate of xerostomia was significantly lower in the IMRT group than in the conventional group.</li>

3035<li>

3036Fatigue was more prevalent in the IMRT group.</li>

3037<li>

3038No significant differences were seen in nonxerostomia late toxicities, locoregional control, or OS at 24 months.</li>

3039</ul>

3040</div>

3041</li>

3042</ol>

3043</div>

3044<section id="_597">

3045<h4 id="_597_toc">

3046Altered fractionation versus standard fractionation radiation therapy</h4>

3047<p id="_598" tabindex="-1">

3048Radiation therapy alone with altered fractionation may be used for patients with locally advanced oropharyngeal cancer who are not candidates for chemotherapy. Altered fractionated radiation therapy yields a higher locoregional control rate than standard fractionated radiation therapy for patients with stage III or stage IV oropharyngeal cancer.</p>

3049<p id="_599" tabindex="-1">

3050Evidence (altered fractionation vs. standard fractionation):</p>

3051<div class="pdq-content-list">

3052<ol id="_600">

3053<li>

3054The randomized <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00771641">

3055RTOG-9003</a>

3056 trial (NCT00771641) included four radiation therapy treatment arms:[<a href="#cit/section_4.21">

305721</a>

3058,<a href="#cit/section_4.22">

305922</a>

3060][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3061Level of evidence A1</a>

3062]<div class="pdq-content-list">

3063<ul id="_601">

3064<li>

3065Standard fractionation (SFX) to 70 Gy in 35 daily fractions for 7 weeks.</li>

3066<li>

3067Hyperfractionation (HFX) to 81.6 Gy in 68 twice-daily fractions for 7 weeks.</li>

3068<li>

3069Accelerated fractionation split course (AFX-S) to 67.2 Gy in 42 fractions for 6 weeks with a 2-week rest after 38.4 Gy.</li>

3070<li>

3071Accelerated concurrent boost fractionation (AFX-C) to 72 Gy in 42 fractions for 6 weeks.</li>

3072</ul>

3073</div>

3074<p id="_602" tabindex="-1">

3075In a long-term analysis, the three investigational arms were compared with SFX.</p>

3076<div class="pdq-content-list">

3077<ul id="_603">

3078<li>

3079Only the HFX arm showed superior locoregional control and survival at 5 years compared with the SFX arm (HR, 0.79; 95% CI, 0.62&#8211;1.00; <em>

3080P</em>

3081 = .05).</li>

3082<li>

3083AFX-C was associated with increased late toxicity compared with SFX.</li>

3084</ul>

3085</div>

3086</li>

3087<li>

3088The following results were shown in a meta-analysis of 15 randomized trials with a total of 6,515 patients and a median follow-up of 6 years involving the assessment of HFX or AFX-S for patients with stage III and stage IV oropharyngeal cancer:[<a href="#cit/section_4.23">

308923</a>

3090][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3091Level of evidence A1</a>

3092]<div class="pdq-content-list">

3093<ul id="_604">

3094<li>

3095There was a significant survival benefit with altered-fractionated radiation therapy and a 3.4% absolute benefit at 5 years (HR, 0.92; 95% CI, 0.86&#8211;0.97; <em>

3096P</em>

3097 = .003). </li>

3098<li>

3099Altered-fractionation radiation therapy improves locoregional control, with greater benefit shown in younger patients.</li>

3100<li>

3101HFX demonstrated a greater survival benefit (8% at 5 years) than did AFX-S (2% with accelerated fractionation without total dose-reduction and 1.7% with total dose-reduction at 5 years, <em>

3102P</em>

3103 = .02).</li>

3104</ul>

3105</div>

3106</li>

3107</ol>

3108</div>

3109</section>

3110<p id="_605" tabindex="-1">

3111An additional late effect from radiation therapy is hypothyroidism, which occurs in 30% to 40% of patients who have received external-beam radiation therapy to the

3112entire thyroid gland. Thyroid function testing of

3113patients is considered before therapy and as part of posttreatment

3114follow-up.[<a href="#cit/section_4.24">

311524</a>

3116,<a href="#cit/section_4.25">

311725</a>

3118]</p>

3119<p id="_625" tabindex="-1">

3120 Prospective data of two randomized controlled trials reported the incidence of hypothyroidism.[<a href="#cit/section_4.26">

312126</a>

3122] </p>

3123<div class="pdq-content-list">

3124<ul id="_668">

3125<li>

3126At a median follow-up of 41 months, 55.1% of the patients developed hypothyroidism (39.3% subclinical, 15.7% biochemical). </li>

3127<li>

3128Patients who underwent IMRT had higher subclinical hypothyroidism (51.1% vs. 27.3%; <em>

3129P</em>

3130 = .021), peaking around 1 year after radiation therapy. </li>

3131<li>

3132Younger age, hypopharynx/larynx primary, node positivity, higher dose/fraction (IMRT arm), and D100 were statistically significant factors for developing hypothyroidism.[<a href="#cit/section_4.26">

313326</a>

3134][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810021&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810021&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3135Level of evidence A3</a>

3136]</li>

3137</ul>

3138</div>

3139<p id="_606" tabindex="-1">

3140For patients with well-lateralized oropharyngeal cancer, such as a T1 or T2 tonsil primary tumor with limited extension into the palate or tongue base, and limited ipsilateral lymph node involvement without extracapsular extension, elective treatment to the ipsilateral lymph nodes results in only minimal risk of spread to the contralateral neck.[<a href="#cit/section_4.27">

314127</a>

3142] For T3 and T4 tumors that are midline or approach the midline, bilateral nodal treatment is a consideration. In addition to the cervical lymph node chain, retropharyngeal lymph nodes can also be encompassed in the elective nodal treatment.</p>

3143</section>

3144<section id="_343">

3145<h3 id="_343_toc">

3146Concurrent Chemoradiation Therapy</h3>

3147<p id="_580" tabindex="-1">

3148Concurrent chemoradiation therapy is a standard treatment option for patients with locally advanced (stage III and stage IV) oropharyngeal carcinoma and is superior to radiation therapy alone.[<a href="#cit/section_4.28">

314928</a>

3150] This treatment approach emphasizes organ preservation and functionality.[<a href="#cit/section_4.29">

315129</a>

3152,<a href="#cit/section_4.30">

315330</a>

3154]</p>

3155<p id="_581" tabindex="-1">

3156Evidence (concurrent chemoradiation therapy):</p>

3157<div class="pdq-content-list">

3158<ol id="_582">

3159<li>

3160A meta-analysis that originally included 93 randomized prospective head and neck cancer trials published between 1965 and

31612000 was updated with the addition of 16 new trials (including 2,767 patients) and 11 updated trials. The results confirmed the benefit and superiority of the addition of concurrent chemotherapy for nonmetastatic head and neck cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_4.31">

316231</a>

3163,<a href="#cit/section_4.32">

316432</a>

3165][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810019&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810019&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3166Level of evidence A2</a>

3167]<div class="pdq-content-list">

3168<ul id="_616">

3169<li>

3170The subset of patients

3171who received chemotherapy and radiation therapy had a 6.5% absolute survival advantage, with OS increasing from 27.7% to 33.6% at 5 years and from 17.3% to 20.9% at 10 years.</li>

3172<li>

3173 Patients who received concurrent chemotherapy had a greater survival benefit than did those who received neoadjuvant chemotherapy.</li>

3174</ul>

3175</div>

3176</li>

3177<li>

3178<p id="_759" tabindex="-1">

3179Postoperative chemoradiation therapy with cisplatin 100 mg/m<span class="sup">

31802</span>

3181 given once every 3 weeks is standard treatment for patients with disease at high risk for recurrence, mainly those with extracapsular lymph node extension and positive surgical margins. However, this dosage has raised concerns about insufficient cisplatin delivery because of high-dose&#8211;related toxicity. Chemoradiation therapy with weekly cisplatin is widely used as an alternative with a better safety profile.</p>

3182<p id="_760" tabindex="-1">

3183A multi-institutional, open-label, noninferiority, phase II/III trial compared different cisplatin schedules as part of postoperative treatment for patients with high-risk locally advanced SCC of the head and neck. Patients received either cisplatin (40 mg/m<span class="sup">

31842</span>

3185) once weekly or standard-dose cisplatin (100 mg/m<span class="sup">

31862</span>

3187) once every 3 weeks, both combined with radiation therapy. OS was the primary end point of the phase III portion of the study. An HR of 1.32 was set as the noninferiority margin. A total of 261 patients were enrolled (cisplatin every 3 weeks, 132 patients; cisplatin weekly, 129 patients).[<a href="#cit/section_4.33">

318833</a>

3189]</p>

3190<div class="pdq-content-list">

3191<ul id="_744">

3192<li>

3193 At the planned third interim analysis in the phase III part of the trial, after a median follow-up of 2.2 years, chemoradiation therapy with weekly cisplatin was noninferior to cisplatin every 3 weeks in terms of OS (HR, 0.69; 99.1% CI, 0.374&#8211;1.273 [&lt;1.32]; one-sided <em>

3194P</em>

3195 for noninferiority = .0027).[<a href="#cit/section_4.33">

319633</a>

3197][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3198Level of evidence A1</a>

3199]</li>

3200<li>

3201 Grade 3 or 4 neutropenia and infection were less frequent in patients who received cisplatin weekly. Grade 3 or 4 neutropenia occurred in 49% of patients who received cisplatin every 3 weeks and 35% of patients who received cisplatin weekly. Grade 3 or 4 infection occurred in 12% of patients who received cisplatin every 3 weeks and 7% of patients who received cisplatin weekly. Grade 3 or 4 renal impairment and hearing impairment were also less frequent in patients who received cisplatin weekly. No treatment-related deaths were reported among patients who received cisplatin every 3 weeks, and two deaths were reported among patients who received weekly cisplatin (1.6%). </li>

3202</ul>

3203</div>

3204<p id="_753" tabindex="-1">

3205Regimens with cisplatin given weekly or every 3 weeks are both considered standard care. A large, randomized, prospective trial is evaluating the equivalent efficacy of these regimens.</p>

3206</li>

3207<li>

3208A phase III randomized trial in India included patients with locally advanced SCC of the head and neck unsuitable for cisplatin-based chemoradiation. This study evaluated using docetaxel as a radiosensitizer. Patients with an Eastern Cooperative Oncology Group (ECOG) performance status (PS) of 0 to 2 were randomly assigned (1:1) to receive either radiation therapy alone or with concurrent docetaxel (15 mg/m<span class="sup">

32092</span>

3210) once weekly for a maximum of seven cycles. The primary end point was 2-year disease-free survival (DFS). Most patients (about 60%) received the treatment as definitive. Cisplatin ineligibility was defined by multiple parameters, including an ECOG PS of 2, calculated creatinine clearance &lt;50 mL/min, and organ dysfunction of grade 2 or higher. A total of 356 patients were enrolled (176 in the radiation therapy arm and 180 in the radiation-and-docetaxel arm).[<a href="#cit/section_4.34">

321134</a>

3212]<div class="pdq-content-list">

3213<ul id="_745">

3214<li>

3215 The 2-year DFS rate was 30.3% in the radiation-alone arm (95% CI, 23.6%&#8211;37.4%) and 42% in the radiation-and-docetaxel arm (95% CI, 34.6%&#8211;49.2%) (HR, 0.673; 95% CI, 0.521&#8211;0.868; <em>

3216P</em>

3217 = .002).</li>

3218<li>

3219 The corresponding median OS was 15.3 months in the radiation-alone arm (95% CI, 13.1&#8211;22.0) and 25.5 months in the radiation-and-docetaxel arm (95% CI, 17.6&#8211;32.5) (log-rank <em>

3220P</em>

3221 = .035). The 2-year OS rate was 41.7% in the radiation-alone arm (95% CI, 34.1%&#8211;49.1%) and 50.8% in the radiation-and-docetaxel arm (95% CI, 43.1%&#8211;58.1%) (HR, 0.747; 95% CI, 0.569&#8211;0.980; <em>

3222P</em>

3223 = .035).[<a href="#cit/section_4.34">

322434</a>

3225][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3226Level of evidence A1</a>

3227]</li>

3228<li>

3229 There was a higher incidence of grade 3 or higher mucositis (22.2% vs. 49.7%; <em>

3230P</em>

3231 &lt; .001), odynophagia (33.5% vs. 52.5%; <em>

3232P</em>

3233 &lt; .001), and dysphagia (33% vs. 49.7%; <em>

3234P</em>

3235 = .002) for patients who received radiation and docetaxel versus radiation alone. </li>

3236<li>

3237 The increase of toxicity observed in the radiation-and-docetaxel arm did not significantly impair the total radiation dose the patients received. A total of 88.9% of patients in the radiation-and-docetaxel arm and 93.8% of patients in the radiation-alone arm received the total radiation dose. In the radiation-and-docetaxel arm, 85.6% of patients received five or more cycles of chemotherapy.</li>

3238</ul>

3239</div>

3240</li>

3241<li>

3242In a randomized trial of patients with locally advanced head and neck cancer, curative-intent radiation therapy alone (213 patients) was compared with radiation therapy plus weekly cetuximab (211 patients).[<a href="#cit/section_4.35">

324335</a>

3244] The initial dose of cetuximab was 400 mg/m<span class="sup">

32452</span>

3246 of body-surface area 1 week before radiation therapy was started, followed by a weekly dose of 250 mg/m<span class="sup">

32472</span>

3248 of body-surface area for the duration of the radiation therapy. This study allowed altered-fractionation regimens to be used in both arms.[<a href="#cit/section_4.35">

324935</a>

3250,<a href="#cit/section_4.36">

325136</a>

3252][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3253Level of evidence A1</a>

3254]<div class="pdq-content-list">

3255<ul id="_617">

3256<li>

3257At a median follow-up of 54 months, patients treated with cetuximab and radiation therapy demonstrated significantly higher progression-free survival (PFS) (HR<sub>

3258death</sub>

3259 or for disease progression, 0.70; <em>

3260P</em>

3261 = .006).</li>

3262<li>

3263Patients in the cetuximab arm experienced higher rates of acneiform rash and infusion reactions, although the incidence of other grade 3 or higher toxicities, including mucositis, did not differ significantly between the two groups.</li>

3264</ul>

3265</div>

3266</li>

3267</ol>

3268</div>

3269<section id="_719">

3270<h4 id="_719_toc">

3271Cetuximab versus cisplatin in patients with human papillomavirus (HPV)-positive oropharyngeal cancer</h4>

3272<p id="_720" tabindex="-1">

3273Studies evaluating de-intensification using reduced-dose radiation therapy (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT02254278">

3274NRG-HN002</a>

3275 [NCT02254278] and <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT01898494">

3276ECOG-3311</a>

3277 [NCT01898494]) are ongoing in patients with low-risk HPV-positive oropharyngeal cancer. Cetuximab, an epidermal growth factor receptor inhibitor, has been evaluated in two randomized trials as a proposed de-intensification strategy to reduce the toxicity of cisplatin-based treatment.</p>

3278<p id="_726" tabindex="-1">

3279Evidence (cetuximab versus cisplatin in patients with HPV-positive oropharyngeal cancer):</p>

3280<div class="pdq-content-list">

3281<ol id="_721">

3282<li>

3283In the randomized <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT01302834">

3284RTOG-1016</a>

3285 trial (NCT01302834), patients with HPV-positive (determined by central confirmation of p16 immunohistochemistry) oropharyngeal cancer were randomly assigned (1:1) to receive either radiation therapy with cetuximab or radiation therapy with cisplatin. This trial aimed to determine whether treatment with radiation therapy and cetuximab produced noninferior survival compared with treatment using radiation therapy and cisplatin. Of the 987 patients enrolled, 849 were randomly assigned to receive radiation therapy plus cetuximab (n = 425) or radiation therapy plus cisplatin (n = 424). Subsequently, 399 patients assigned to receive cetuximab and 406 patients assigned to receive cisplatin were determined to be eligible. Patients received 70 Gy of radiation therapy in 6 weeks accelerated (six fractions/week) with either two cycles of cisplatin (100 mg/m<span class="sup">

32862</span>

3287) every 3 weeks or weekly cetuximab.[<a href="#cit/section_4.37">

328837</a>

3289][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3290Level of evidence A1</a>

3291]<div class="pdq-content-list">

3292<ul id="_722">

3293<li>

3294 After a median follow-up duration of 4.5 years, radiation therapy plus cetuximab did not meet the noninferiority criteria for OS (HR, 1.45; one-sided 95% upper CI, 1.94; <em>

3295P</em>

3296 = .5056 for noninferiority; one-sided log-rank <em>

3297P</em>

3298 = .0163).[<a href="#cit/section_4.38">

329938</a>

3300][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3301Level of evidence A1</a>

3302] </li>

3303<li>

3304 The estimated 5-year OS rate was 77.9% (95% CI, 73.4%&#8211;82.5%) in the cetuximab group versus 84.6% (80.6%&#8211;88.6%) in the cisplatin group. </li>

3305<li>

3306 PFS was significantly lower in the cetuximab group than in the cisplatin group (HR, 1.72; 95% CI, 1.29&#8211;2.29; <em>

3307P</em>

3308 = .0002; 5-year PFS rate 67.3%; 95% CI, 62.4%&#8211;72.2% vs. 78.4%, 73.8%&#8211;83.0%), and locoregional failure was significantly higher in the cetuximab group than in the cisplatin group. </li>

3309<li>

3310 Acute moderate to severe toxicity (77.4%, 95% CI, 73.0%&#8211;81.5% vs. 81.7%, 77.5%&#8211;85.3%; <em>

3311P</em>

3312 = .1586) and late moderate to severe toxicity (16.5%, 95% CI, 12.9%&#8211;20.7% vs. 20.4%, 16.4%&#8211;24.8%; <em>

3313P</em>

3314 = .1904) were similar between the cetuximab and cisplatin groups. </li>

3315</ul>

3316</div>

3317</li>

3318<li>

3319<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT01874171">

3320De-ESCALaTE HPV</a>

3321 [NCT01874171] was an open-label, randomized, controlled, phase III trial at 32 head and neck treatment centers in Ireland, the Netherlands, and the United Kingdom. It included patients aged 18 years or older with HPV-positive, low-risk oropharyngeal cancer (nonsmokers or lifetime smokers with a smoking history of &lt;10 pack-years).[<a href="#cit/section_4.39">

332239</a>

3323] Patients were randomly assigned (1:1) to receive, in addition to radiation therapy (70 Gy in 35 fractions), either intravenous cisplatin (100 mg/m<span class="sup">

33242</span>

3325 on days 1, 22, and 43 of radiation therapy) or intravenous cetuximab (400 mg/m<span class="sup">

33262</span>

3327 loading dose followed by seven weekly infusions of 250 mg/m<span class="sup">

33282</span>

3329). <p id="_724" tabindex="-1">

3330The primary outcome was overall severe (grades 3&#8211;5) toxicity events at 24 months from the end of treatment. The primary outcome was assessed by intention-to-treat and per-protocol analyses. Between Nov 12, 2012, and Oct 1, 2016, 334 patients were recruited (166 in the cisplatin group and 168 in the cetuximab group).</p>

3331<div class="pdq-content-list">

3332<ul id="_725">

3333<li>

3334 Overall (acute and late) severe (grades 3&#8211;5) toxicity did not differ significantly between treatment groups at 24 months (mean number of events per patient, 4.8 [95% CI, 4.2&#8211;5.4] with cisplatin vs. 4.8 [4.2&#8211;5.4] with cetuximab; <em>

3335P</em>

3336 = .98).[<a href="#cit/section_4.39">

333739</a>

3338][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3339Level of evidence A1</a>

3340]</li>

3341<li>

3342 At 24 months, overall all-grade toxicity did not differ significantly (mean number of events per patient, 29.2 [95% CI, 27.3&#8211;31.0] with cisplatin vs. 30.1 [28.3&#8211;31.9] with cetuximab; <em>

3343P</em>

3344 = .49).</li>

3345<li>

3346 OS was inferior and local recurrence rate was higher in the cetuximab arm. The 2-year OS rate was 97.5% for the cisplatin group versus 89.4% for the cetuximab group (HR, 5.0; 95% CI, 1.7&#8211;14.7; <em>

3347P</em>

3348 = .001), and the 2-year recurrence rate was 6.0% for the cisplatin group versus 16.1% for the cetuximab group (HR, 3.4; 1.6&#8211;7.2; <em>

3349P</em>

3350 = .0007). </li>

3351</ul>

3352</div>

3353</li>

3354</ol>

3355</div>

3356</section>

3357<p id="_713" tabindex="-1">

3358These findings showed the inferiority of cetuximab compared with cisplatin for OS and local recurrence rates for patients with locoregionally advanced HPV-related oropharyngeal cancer and also did not demonstrate reduced toxicity with cetuximab and radiation therapy compared with cisplatin. Treatment with the combination of radiation therapy and cetuximab resulted in inferior OS and PFS compared with treatment using radiation therapy and cisplatin; therefore, treatment with radiation therapy and cisplatin remains the standard of care.</p>

3359<p id="_618" tabindex="-1">

3360For more information about oral toxicities, see <a href="/about-cancer/treatment/side-effects/mouth-throat/oral-complications-hp-pdq">

3361Oral Complications of Chemotherapy and Head/Neck Radiation</a>

3362.</p>

3363</section>

3364<section id="_562">

3365<h3 id="_562_toc">

3366Neoadjuvant Chemotherapy Followed by Concurrent Chemoradiation Therapy</h3>

3367<p id="_563" tabindex="-1">

3368In a meta-analysis of five randomized trials, a total of 1,022 patients with locally advanced head and neck SCC were assigned to receive either neoadjuvant chemotherapy with TPF (docetaxel, cisplatin, and fluorouracil) followed by concurrent chemoradiation therapy or concurrent chemoradiation therapy alone. The analysis failed to show an OS (HR, 1.01; 95% confidence limits [CLs], 0.84, 1.21; <em>

3369P</em>

3370 = .92) or PFS (HR, 0.91; 95% CLs, 0.75, 1.1; <em>

3371P</em>

3372 = .32) advantage for neoadjuvant chemotherapy using the TPF regimen over concurrent chemoradiation therapy alone.[<a href="#cit/section_4.40">

337340</a>

3374][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3375Level of evidence A1</a>

3376]</p>

3377<p id="_620" tabindex="-1">

3378Evidence (neoadjuvant chemotherapy followed by concurrent chemoradiation therapy):</p>

3379<div class="pdq-content-list">

3380<ol id="_621">

3381<li>

3382In a phase II study of neoadjuvant chemotherapy using cisplatin, paclitaxel, and cetuximab in patients with HPV-associated oropharyngeal SCC (ECOG 1308 [<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT01084083">

3383NCT01084083</a>

3384]), patients who achieved a complete clinical response to three cycles of neoadjuvant chemotherapy received reduced-dose IMRT of 54 Gy with weekly cetuximab. Patients with less than a clinical complete response received 69.3 Gy of radiation to the primary site or nodes and cetuximab.[<a href="#cit/section_4.41">

338541</a>

3386]<div class="pdq-content-list">

3387<ul id="_622">

3388<li>

3389With a median follow-up of 35.4 months, the 2-year PFS rate was 80% and the OS rate was 94% for patients who achieved a complete clinical response and were treated with 54 Gy of radiation.</li>

3390<li>

3391For patients whose primary tumor (T) was less than T4 and regional lymph nodes (N) were less than N2c and had a smoking history of less than 10 pack-years, the 2-year PFS rate was 96% and the OS rate was 96%. </li>

3392<li>

3393Lower-dose radiation using 54 Gy was associated with lower rates of dysphagia with solid foods and less-impaired nutrition. </li>

3394<li>

3395There does not appear to be a benefit from treatment with neoadjuvant chemotherapy followed by full-dose (&#8805;70 Gy) concurrent chemoradiation therapy. There may be a role for radiation dose de-escalation in HPV-positive patients with low-risk disease who achieve a complete clinical response after neoadjuvant chemotherapy.</li>

3396</ul>

3397</div>

3398</li>

3399</ol>

3400</div>

3401<p id="_619" tabindex="-1">

3402 Overall, the role of neoadjuvant chemotherapy for patients with oropharyngeal cancer remains unclear. However, in HPV-defined subsets, more information is needed because, as this phase II study suggests, in that setting, neoadjuvant chemotherapy may be used with less chemoradiation.[<a href="#cit/section_4.40">

340340</a>

3404,<a href="#cit/section_4.42">

340542</a>

3406-<a href="#cit/section_4.45">

340745</a>

3408][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3409Level of evidence A1</a>

3410]</p>

3411</section>

3620<section id="_472">

3621<h3 id="_472_toc">

3622Treatment Options for Stage I and Stage II Oropharyngeal Cancer</h3>

3623<p id="_474" tabindex="-1">

3624The management of stage I and stage II carcinomas of the oropharynx requires multidisciplinary input to establish the optimal treatment. Radiation therapy or surgery is equally successful in controlling stage I and stage II oropharyngeal cancer. For more information, see the <a href="/types/head-and-neck/hp/adult/oropharyngeal-treatment-pdq#_49">

3625Treatment Option Overview for Oropharyngeal Cancer</a>

3626 section.</p>

3627<p id="_475" tabindex="-1">

3628The choice of treatment is dictated by the anticipated functional speech and swallowing and cosmetic outcome of the treatment options and by the available expertise of the surgeon or radiation oncologist. Treatment is individualized for each patient.</p>

3629<p id="_476" tabindex="-1">

3630Treatment options for <a href="/types/head-and-neck/hp/adult/oropharyngeal-treatment-pdq#_701">

3631stage I</a>

3632 and <a href="/types/head-and-neck/hp/adult/oropharyngeal-treatment-pdq#_702">

3633stage II</a>

3634 oropharyngeal cancer include the following:</p>

3635<div class="pdq-content-list">

3636<ol id="_477">

3637<li>

3638<a href="/types/head-and-neck/hp/adult/oropharyngeal-treatment-pdq#_337">

3639Radiation therapy using standard fractionation</a>

3640.</li>

3641<li>

3642<a href="/types/head-and-neck/hp/adult/oropharyngeal-treatment-pdq#_334">

3643Surgery</a>

3644.

3645</li>

3646</ol>

3647</div>

3648</section>

3649<section id="_TrialSearch_55_sid_5">

3650<h3 id="_TrialSearch_55_sid_5_toc">

3651Current Clinical Trials</h3>

3652<p id="_TrialSearch_55_22" tabindex="-1">

3653Use our <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search/advanced" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search/advanced">

3654advanced clinical trial search</a>

3655 to find NCI-supported cancer clinical trials that are now enrolling patients. The search can be narrowed by location of the trial, type of treatment, name of the drug, and other criteria. <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/">

3656General information</a>

3657 about clinical trials is also available.</p>

37074</a>

3737RTOG 3504</a>

3738 (NCT02764593) evaluated concurrent chemoradiation therapy with nivolumab in patients with intermediate- to high-risk head and neck cancer. </li>

3741NRG-HN002</a>

3834

3835 <div id="_section_104" class="pdq-sections">

3836<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

3837<h6>

3838In This Section</h6>

3839<ul>

3840<li>

3841<a href="#_512_toc">

3842Treatment Options for Metastatic and Recurrent Oropharyngeal Cancer<ul>

3843<li>

3844<a href="#_669_toc">

3845Chemotherapy</a>

3846</li>

3847<li>

3848<a href="#_635_toc">

3849Immunotherapy</a>

3850</li>

3851</ul>

3852</a>
