3860<section id="_512">

3861<h3 id="_512_toc">

3862Treatment Options for Metastatic and Recurrent Oropharyngeal Cancer</h3>

3863<p id="_629" tabindex="-1">

3864The management of metastatic and recurrent carcinomas of the oropharynx is complex and

3865requires multidisciplinary input to establish the optimal treatment. For more information, see the <a href="/types/head-and-neck/hp/adult/oropharyngeal-treatment-pdq#_49">

3866Treatment Option Overview for Oropharyngeal Cancer</a>

3867 section.</p>

3868<p id="_513" tabindex="-1">

3869Treatment options for metastatic and recurrent oropharyngeal cancer include the following:</p>

3870<div class="pdq-content-list">

3871<ol id="_514">

3872<li>

3873<a href="/types/head-and-neck/hp/adult/oropharyngeal-treatment-pdq#_334">

3874Surgical resection</a>

3875, if technically feasible and the tumor does not respond to radiation therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_7.1">

38761</a>

3877]

3878</li>

3879<li>

3880<a href="/types/head-and-neck/hp/adult/oropharyngeal-treatment-pdq#_337">

3881Radiation therapy</a>

3882, if the tumor is not completely removed by surgery and curative doses of radiation have not been given previously.[<a href="#cit/section_7.2">

38832</a>

3884]</li>

3885<li>

3886A second <a href="/types/head-and-neck/hp/adult/oropharyngeal-treatment-pdq#_334">

3887surgery</a>

3888, if the tumor was not completely removed initially and if technically feasible.[<a href="#cit/section_7.1">

38891</a>

3890]

3891</li>

3892<li>

3893<a href="/types/head-and-neck/hp/adult/oropharyngeal-treatment-pdq#_669">

3894Chemotherapy</a>

3895, for unresectable locoregionally recurrent disease.</li>

3896<li>

3897Additional <a href="/types/head-and-neck/hp/adult/oropharyngeal-treatment-pdq#_337">

3898radiation therapy</a>

3899 using conventionally fractionated radiation therapy, or hyperfractionated radiation therapy (HFX) with concurrent chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_7.3">

39003</a>

3901]</li>

3902<li>

3903Stereotactic body radiation therapy with concurrent cetuximab.[<a href="#cit/section_7.4">

39044</a>

3905] </li>

3906<li>

3907<a href="/types/head-and-neck/hp/adult/oropharyngeal-treatment-pdq#_635">

3908 Immunotherapy</a>

3909 (inhibitor of the programmed death-ligand 1 [PD-L1] pathway) can be used after platinum-based chemotherapy failure in the locally metastatic or recurrent setting.[<a href="#cit/section_7.5">

39105</a>

3911,<a href="#cit/section_7.6">

39126</a>

3913]</li>

3914<li>

3915Clinical trials evaluating additional radiation therapy using HFX with concurrent chemotherapy, targeted therapy, stereotactic body radiation therapy, or immunotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_7.7">

39167</a>

3917]</li>

3918</ol>

3919</div>

3920<section id="_669">

3921<h4 id="_669_toc">

3922Chemotherapy</h4>

3923<p id="_670" tabindex="-1">

3924Platinum-based chemotherapy is often used as first-line treatment for patients with metastatic or recurrent squamous cell carcinoma (SCC) of the head and neck. </p>

3925<p id="_671" tabindex="-1">

3926Evidence (chemotherapy):</p>

3927<div class="pdq-content-list">

3928<ol id="_672">

3929<li>

3930In a phase III randomized trial of 442 patients with untreated metastatic or recurrent SCC of the head and neck, adding cetuximab to platinum plus fluorouracil improved overall survival (OS), compared with platinum plus fluorouracil alone. The median survival was 10.1 months versus 7.4 months (hazard ratio [HR] for death [HR<sub>

3931death</sub>

3932], 0.80; 95% confidence interval [CI], 0.64&#8211;0.99; <em>

3933P</em>

3934 = .04).[<a href="#cit/section_7.8">

39358</a>

3936]<div class="pdq-content-list">

3937<ul id="_673">

3938<li>

3939Quality of life (QOL) was not adversely affected by adding cetuximab to this platinum-based regimen.[<a href="#cit/section_7.9">

39409</a>

3941]</li>

3942</ul>

3943</div>

3944<p id="_674" tabindex="-1">

3945Tumor <em class="gene-name">

3946EGFR</em>

3947 gene copy number was not a predictive biomarker for the efficacy of cetuximab plus platinum and fluorouracil as first-line therapy for patients with metastatic or recurrent SCC of the head and neck.[<a href="#cit/section_7.10">

394810</a>

3949][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3950Level of evidence A1</a>

3951]</p>

3952</li>

3953<li>

3954An open-label, phase III, randomized trial demonstrated improved progression-free survival (PFS) for patients who received afatinib compared with patients who received methotrexate.[<a href="#cit/section_7.11">

395511</a>

3956]<div class="pdq-content-list">

3957<ol id="_675" class="lower-alpha">

3958<li>

3959After a median follow-up of 6.7 months, the median PFS was 2.6 months (95% CI, 2.0&#8211;2.7) for the afatinib group and 1.7 months (95% CI, 1.5&#8211;2.4) for the methotrexate group (HR, 0.80 [95% CI, 0.65&#8211;0.98]; <em>

3960P</em>

3961 = .030).</li>

3962<li>

3963The most frequent grade 3 or grade 4 drug-related adverse events for patients treated with afatinib or methotrexate included the following: <div class="pdq-content-list">

3964<ul id="_676">

3965<li>

3966Rash or acne (10% for afatinib vs. 0% for methotrexate).</li>

3967<li>

3968Diarrhea (9% for afatinib vs. 2% for methotrexate).</li>

3969<li>

3970Stomatitis (6% for afatinib vs. 8% for methotrexate).</li>

3971<li>

3972Fatigue (6% for afatinib vs. 3% for methotrexate).</li>

3973<li>

3974Neutropenia (&lt;1% for afatinib vs. 7% for methotrexate).</li>

3975</ul>

3976</div>

3977</li>

3978<li>

3979Overall, serious adverse events occurred in 14% of patients treated with afatinib and 11% of patients treated with methotrexate.</li>

3980</ol>

3981</div>

3982</li>

3983</ol>

3984</div>

3985</section>

3986<section id="_635">

3987<h4 id="_635_toc">

3988Immunotherapy</h4>

3989<section id="_677">

3990<p id="_678" tabindex="-1">

3991<strong>

3992Pembrolizumab</strong>

3993</p>

3994<p id="_679" tabindex="-1">

3995Pembrolizumab is a monoclonal antibody and an inhibitor of the programmed death (PD-1) pathway. There are two studies evaluating pembrolizumab in patients with incurable metastatic or recurrent head and neck squamous cell carcinoma (SCC).</p>

3996<p id="_680" tabindex="-1">

3997Evidence (pembrolizumab as first-line therapy):</p>

3998<div class="pdq-content-list">

3999<ol id="_714">

4000<li>

4001 Performed at 200 sites in 37 countries, <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT02358031">

4002KEYNOTE-048</a>

4003 [NCT02358031] was a nonblinded, randomized, phase III study of participants with untreated locally incurable metastatic or recurrent head and neck SCC.[<a href="#cit/section_7.12">

400412</a>

4005] Patients were randomly assigned (1:1:1) to receive pembrolizumab alone, pembrolizumab plus a platinum and fluorouracil (pembrolizumab with chemotherapy), or cetuximab plus a platinum and fluorouracil (cetuximab with chemotherapy). Investigators, participants, and representatives of the sponsor were masked to the PD-L1 combined positive score (CPS) results; PD-L1 positivity was not required for study entry.<p id="_715" tabindex="-1">

4006The primary end points were OS and PFS. Progression was defined as radiographically confirmed disease progression or death from any cause, whichever came first, in the intention-to-treat. The trial allocated 882 participants to receive pembrolizumab alone (n = 301), pembrolizumab with chemotherapy (n = 281), or cetuximab with chemotherapy (n = 300); of these, 754 (85%) had a CPS of 1 or higher and 381 (43%) had a CPS of 20 or higher.</p>

4007<div class="pdq-content-list">

4008<ul id="_716">

4009<li>

4010 At second interim analysis, pembrolizumab alone improved OS versus cetuximab with chemotherapy in the population with a CPS of 20 or higher (median, 14.9 months vs. 10.7 months, HR, 0.61; 95% CI, 0.45&#8211;0.83; <em>

4011P</em>

4012 = .0007) and the population with a CPS of 1 or higher (12.3 months vs. 10.3 months, HR, 0.78; 0.64&#8211;0.96, <em>

4013P</em>

4014 = .0086) and was noninferior in the total population (11.6 months vs. 10.7 months, HR, 0.85; 0.71&#8211;1.03).[<a href="#cit/section_7.12">

401512</a>

4016][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4017Level of evidence A1</a>

4018] </li>

4019<li>

4020 Pembrolizumab with chemotherapy improved OS versus cetuximab with chemotherapy in the total population (13.0 months vs. 10.7 months, HR, 0.77; 95% CI, 0.63&#8211;0.93, <em>

4021P</em>

4022 = .0034) at the second interim analysis and in the population with a CPS of 20 or higher (14.7 months vs. 11.0 months, HR, 0.60; 0.45&#8211;0.82, <em>

4023P</em>

4024 = .0004) and the population with a CPS of 1 or higher (13.6 months vs. 10.4 months, HR, 0.65; 0.53&#8211;0.80, <em>

4025P</em>

4026 &lt; .0001) at final analysis. </li>

4027<li>

4028 Neither pembrolizumab alone nor pembrolizumab with chemotherapy improved PFS at the second interim analysis. </li>

4029<li>

4030 At final analysis, grade 3 or worse all-cause adverse events occurred in 164 of 300 treated participants (55%) in the pembrolizumab-alone group, 235 of 276 (85%) in the group receiving pembrolizumab with chemotherapy, and 239 of 287 (83%) in the group receiving cetuximab with chemotherapy. </li>

4031<li>

4032 Adverse events led to death in 25 participants (8%) in the pembrolizumab-alone group, 32 (12%) in the group receiving pembrolizumab with chemotherapy, and 28 (10%) in the group receiving cetuximab with chemotherapy.</li>

4033</ul>

4034</div>

4035</li>

4036</ol>

4037</div>

4038<p id="_717" tabindex="-1">

4039Pembrolizumab plus platinum and fluorouracil is an appropriate first-line treatment for patients with metastatic or recurrent head and neck SCC. Pembrolizumab monotherapy is an appropriate first-line treatment for patients with PD-L1&#8211;positive metastatic or recurrent head and neck SCC.</p>

4040<p id="_718" tabindex="-1">

4041Evidence (pembrolizumab after progression on platinum-based treatment):</p>

4042<div class="pdq-content-list">

4043<ol id="_681">

4044<li>

4045<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT02252042">

4046KEYNOTE-040</a>

4047 [NCT02252042], a randomized phase III trial, included patients with incurable metastatic or recurrent head and neck SCC who had received platinum-based treatment within 3 to 6 months.[<a href="#cit/section_7.5">

40485</a>

4049] Patients were randomly assigned to the pembrolizumab arm (200 mg every 3 weeks [247 patients]) or to the standard therapy arm of the investigator&#8217;s choice (methotrexate, docetaxel, or cetuximab [248 patients]). Patients received treatment until progression or toxicity. The maximum duration of pembrolizumab was 24 months. The primary end point was OS in the intention-to-treat population. <div class="pdq-content-list">

4050<ul id="_682">

4051<li>

4052 The median OS was 8.4 months in the pembrolizumab arm versus 6.9 months in the standard therapy arm (HR, 0.80 [0.65&#8211;0.98]; nominal <em>

4053P</em>

4054 = .0161).[<a href="#cit/section_7.5">

40555</a>

4056][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4057Level of evidence A1</a>

4058]</li>

4059<li>

4060 Pembrolizumab was associated with fewer grade 3 or higher adverse events (pembrolizumab, 13% vs. standard therapy, 36%). The most common treatment-related adverse events were hypothyroidism (13%) in the pembrolizumab arm and fatigue (18%) in the standard therapy arm. </li>

4061<li>

4062 In patients receiving pembrolizumab, there were four treatment-related deaths resulting from large intestinal perforation, Stevens-Johnson syndrome, and unspecified malignant progression; two treatment-related deaths in the standard therapy arm resulted from malignant progression and pneumonia. </li>

4063<li>

4064 The PD-L1 combined positive score was 1 or higher in 79% of the patients in the pembrolizumab arm and 77% of the patients in the standard therapy arm. </li>

4065<li>

4066 Patients with PD-1 expression on their tumors or in the tumor microenvironment as noted by the PD-L1 combined positive score of 1 or higher (HR, 0.74; 95% CI, 0.58&#8211;0.93; nominal <em>

4067P</em>

4068 = .0049) or a PD-L1 tumor proportion score of 50% or higher (HR, 0.53; 95% CI, 0.35&#8211;0.81; nominal <em>

4069P</em>

4070 = .0014), and treated with pembrolizumab had reduced HR<sub>

4071death</sub>

4072 when compared with patients treated with standard therapy. </li>

4073</ul>

4074</div>

4075</li>

4076</ol>

4077</div>

4078</section>

4079<section id="_636">

4080<p id="_683" tabindex="-1">

4081<strong>

4082Nivolumab</strong>

4083</p>

4084<p id="_637" tabindex="-1">

4085Nivolumab is a fully human immunoglobulin G4 (IgG4) anti&#8211;PD-1 monoclonal antibody.</p>

4086<p id="_638" tabindex="-1">

4087Evidence (nivolumab):</p>

4088<div class="pdq-content-list">

4089<ol id="_639">

4090<li>

4091An open-label, phase III trial, randomized in a 2:1 ratio, included 361 patients with recurrent SCC of the head and neck and disease progression within 6 months after platinum-based chemotherapy. Patients were assigned to receive nivolumab (at a dose of 3 mg/kg of body weight) every 2 weeks or standard single-agent systemic therapy (methotrexate, docetaxel, or cetuximab). The primary end point was OS.[<a href="#cit/section_7.6">

40926</a>

4093]<div class="pdq-content-list">

4094<ul id="_640">

4095<li>

4096 The median OS was 7.5 months (95% CI, 5.5&#8211;9.1) in the nivolumab group versus 5.1 months (95% CI, 4.0&#8211;6.0) in the standard therapy arm. OS was significantly longer with nivolumab than with the standard therapy (HR<sub>

4097death</sub>

4098, 0.70; 97.73% CI, 0.51&#8211;0.96; <em>

4099P</em>

4100 = .01), and the estimates of the 1-year survival rate were approximately 19% higher with nivolumab (36.0%) than with standard therapy (16.6%).[<a href="#cit/section_7.6">

41016</a>

4102][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4103Level of evidence A1</a>

4104]</li>

4105<li>

4106 There was no difference in median PFS between arms. The rate of PFS at 6 months was 19.7% with nivolumab versus 9.9% with standard therapy. </li>

4107<li>

4108 The response rate was 13.3% in the nivolumab group versus 5.8% in the standard therapy group.</li>

4109<li>

4110 Grade 3 or 4 treatment-related adverse events occurred in 13.1% of the patients in the nivolumab group compared with 35.1% in the standard therapy group. </li>

4111<li>

4112 QOL using the European Organisation for Research and Treatment of Cancer Quality of Life Questionnaire (QLQ), Core Module (QLQ-C30) and the Head and Neck Module (QLQ-H&amp;N35) domains&#8212;including physical, role, social functioning, pain, sensory, and social contact problems&#8212;were stable in the nivolumab arm, but QOL was worse in the standard therapy arm. </li>

4113<li>

4114 In the subgroup of patients with a PD-L1 expression level of 1% or higher, the HR<sub>

4115death</sub>

4116 among the patients treated with nivolumab versus standard therapy was 0.55 (95% CI, 0.36&#8211;0.83) compared with the subgroup of patients with a PD-L1 expression level of lower than 1% for whom the HR was 0.89 (95% CI, 0.54&#8211;1.45; <em>

4117P</em>

4118 = .17 for interaction).

4119

4120</li>

4121</ul>

4122</div>

4123</li>

4124<li>

4125A randomized, phase III, superiority study in India evaluated the dose of immune checkpoint inhibitors in the setting of palliative care for patients with advanced head and neck cancer. Low-dose intravenous nivolumab (20 mg every 3 weeks) was added to a triple metronomic chemotherapy regimen of oral methotrexate (9 mg/m<span class="sup">

41262</span>

4127 once weekly), celecoxib (200 mg twice daily), and erlotinib (150 mg once daily). Notably, this nivolumab dose is less than 10% of the dose recommended by the U.S. Food and Drug Administration and the European Medicines Agency. A total of 151 patients were randomly assigned to receive either triple metronomic chemotherapy alone (n = 75) or triple metronomic chemotherapy with nivolumab (n = 76). The primary end point was 1-year OS.[<a href="#cit/section_7.13">

412813</a>

4129] <div class="pdq-content-list">

4130<ul id="_746">

4131<li>

4132 The addition of low-dose nivolumab to triple metronomic chemotherapy improved the 1-year OS rate from 16.3% (95% CI, 8.0%&#8211;27.4%) to 43.4% (95% CI, 30.8%&#8211;55.3%) (HR, 0.545; 95% CI, 0.362&#8211;0.820; <em>

4133P</em>

4134 = .0036).[<a href="#cit/section_7.13">

413513</a>

4136][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4137Level of evidence A1</a>

4138]</li>

4139<li>

4140 The median OS was 6.7 months (95% CI, 5.8&#8211;8.1) for patients who received triple metronomic chemotherapy alone and 10.1 months (95% CI, 7.4&#8211;12.6) for patients who received triple metronomic chemotherapy with nivolumab (<em>

4141P</em>

4142 = .0052). </li>

4143<li>

4144 The rate of grade 3 or higher adverse events was 50% for patients who received triple metronomic chemotherapy alone and 46.1% for patients who received triple metronomic chemotherapy with nivolumab (<em>

4145P</em>

4146 = .744). </li>

4147</ul>

4148</div>

4149<p id="_747" tabindex="-1">

4150Although the control arm in this study cannot be considered standard care, lower doses of immunotherapy appeared to have some benefit in this setting.[<a href="#cit/section_7.14">

415114</a>

4152]</p>

4153</li>

4154</ol>

4155</div>

4156</section>

Lancet 394 (10212): 1915-1928, 2019.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=31679945&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=31679945&amp;dopt=Abstract">

4218[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

4219</li>

4220<li id="section_7.13">

4221Patil VM, Noronha V, Menon N, et al.: Low-Dose Immunotherapy in Head and Neck Cancer: A Randomized Study. J Clin Oncol 41 (2): 222-232, 2023.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=36265101&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=36265101&amp;dopt=Abstract">

4222[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

4223</li>

4224<li id="section_7.14">

4225Mitchell AP, Goldstein DA: Cost Savings and Increased Access With Ultra-Low-Dose Immunotherapy. J Clin Oncol 41 (2): 170-172, 2023.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=36265102&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=36265102&amp;dopt=Abstract">

4226[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

4227</li>

4228</ol>

4229</div>

4230

4231 </section>

4232

4233

4234

4235 <section id="_133">

4236

4237 <h2>

4238Changes to This Summary (09/05/2023)</h2>

4239

4240 <div id="_section_133" class="pdq-sections">

4241<p id="_134" tabindex="-1">

4242The PDQ cancer information summaries are reviewed regularly and updated as new information becomes available. This section describes the latest changes made to this summary as of the date above.</p>

4243<p id="_749" tabindex="-1">

4244<strong>

4245<a href="/types/head-and-neck/hp/adult/oropharyngeal-treatment-pdq#_49">

4246Treatment Option Overview for Oropharyngeal Cancer</a>

4247</strong>

4248</p>

4249<p id="_756" tabindex="-1">

4250Revised <a href="/types/head-and-neck/hp/adult/oropharyngeal-treatment-pdq#_529">

4251Table 11</a>

4252, Treatment Options for Oropharyngeal Cancer.</p>

4253<p id="_750" tabindex="-1">

4254Revised <a href="/types/head-and-neck/hp/adult/oropharyngeal-treatment-pdq#_582">

4255text</a>

4256 to state that a meta-analysis that originally included 93 randomized prospective head and neck cancer trials published between 1965 and 2000 was updated with the addition of 16 new trials and 11 updated trials. The results confirmed the benefit and superiority of the addition of concurrent chemotherapy for nonmetastatic head and neck cancer (cited Lacas et al. as reference 32 and level of evidence A2).</p>

4257<p id="_751" tabindex="-1">

4258Added <a href="/types/head-and-neck/hp/adult/oropharyngeal-treatment-pdq#_759">

4259text</a>

4260 to state that postoperative chemoradiation therapy with cisplatin 100 mg/m<span class="sup">

42612</span>

4262 given once every 3 weeks is standard treatment for patients with disease at high risk for recurrence, mainly those with extracapsular lymph node extension and positive surgical margins. However, this dosage has raised concerns about insufficient cisplatin delivery because of high-dose&#8211;related toxicity. Chemoradiation therapy with weekly cisplatin is widely used as an alternative with a better safety profile.</p>

4263<p id="_752" tabindex="-1">

4264Added <a href="/types/head-and-neck/hp/adult/oropharyngeal-treatment-pdq#_760">

4265text</a>

4266 about a multi-institutional, open-label, noninferiority, phase II/III trial that compared different cisplatin schedules as part of postoperative treatment for patients with high-risk locally advanced squamous cell carcinoma (SCC) of the head and neck. The phase III portion of the study showed that, when given with radiation therapy, weekly cisplatin was noninferior to cisplatin every 3 weeks in terms of overall survival (cited Kiyota et al. as reference 33 and level of evidence A1).</p>

4267<p id="_754" tabindex="-1">

4268Added <a href="/types/head-and-neck/hp/adult/oropharyngeal-treatment-pdq#_753">

4269text</a>

4270 to state that regimens with cisplatin given weekly or every 3 weeks are both considered standard care. A large, randomized, prospective trial is evaluating the equivalent efficacy of these regimens.</p>

4271<p id="_755" tabindex="-1">

4272Added <a href="/types/head-and-neck/hp/adult/oropharyngeal-treatment-pdq#_753">

4273text</a>

4274 about the results of a phase III randomized trial in India that included patients with locally advanced SCC of the head and neck unsuitable for cisplatin-based chemoradiation. This study evaluated radiation therapy alone or with concurrent docetaxel and had a primary end point of 2-year disease-free survival (cited Patil et al. as reference 34 and level of evidence A1).</p>

4275<p id="_757" tabindex="-1">

4276<strong>

4277<a href="/types/head-and-neck/hp/adult/oropharyngeal-treatment-pdq#_104">

4278Treatment of Metastatic and Recurrent Oropharyngeal Cancer</a>

4279</strong>

4280</p>

4281<p id="_758" tabindex="-1">

4282Added <a href="/types/head-and-neck/hp/adult/oropharyngeal-treatment-pdq#_638">

4283text</a>

4284 about the results of a randomized, phase III, superiority study that evaluated the addition of low-dose intravenous nivolumab to a triple metronomic chemotherapy regimen as palliative care for patients with advanced head and neck cancer (cited Patil et al. as reference 13 and level of evidence A1). Although the control arm in this study cannot be considered standard care, lower doses of immunotherapy appeared to have some benefit in this setting (cited Mitchell et al. as reference 14).</p>

4285<p id="_disclaimerHP_3" tabindex="-1">

4286This summary is written and maintained by the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/editorial-boards/adult-treatment" title="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/editorial-boards/adult-treatment">

4287PDQ Adult Treatment Editorial Board</a>

4288, which is

4289editorially independent of NCI. The summary reflects an independent review of

4290the literature and does not represent a policy statement of NCI or NIH. More

4291information about summary policies and the role of the PDQ Editorial Boards in

4292maintaining the PDQ summaries can be found on the <a href="/types/head-and-neck/hp/adult/oropharyngeal-treatment-pdq#_AboutThis_1">

4293About This PDQ Summary</a>

4294 and <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq" title="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq">

4295PDQ&#174; Cancer Information for Health Professionals</a>

4296 pages.

4297</p>

4298</div>

4299

4300 </section>

4303

4304 <section id="_AboutThis_1">

4305

4306 <h2>

4307About This PDQ Summary</h2>

4308

4309 <div id="_section_AboutThis_1" class="pdq-sections">

4310<section id="_AboutThis_2">

4311<h3 id="_AboutThis_2_toc">

4312Purpose of This Summary</h3>

4313<p id="_AboutThis_3" tabindex="-1">

4314This PDQ cancer information summary for health professionals provides comprehensive, peer-reviewed, evidence-based information about the treatment of adult oropharyngeal cancer. It is intended as a resource to inform and assist clinicians in the care of their patients. It does not provide formal guidelines or recommendations for making health care decisions.</p>

4315</section>

4316<section id="_AboutThis_4">

4317<h3 id="_AboutThis_4_toc">

4318Reviewers and Updates</h3>

4319<p id="_AboutThis_5" tabindex="-1">

4320This summary is reviewed regularly and updated as necessary by the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/editorial-boards/adult-treatment" title="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/editorial-boards/adult-treatment">

4321PDQ Adult Treatment Editorial Board</a>

4322, which is editorially independent of the National Cancer Institute (NCI). The summary reflects an independent review of the literature and does not represent a policy statement of NCI or the National Institutes of Health (NIH).</p>

4323<p id="_AboutThis_22" tabindex="-1">

4324 Board members review recently published articles each month to determine whether an article should:</p>

4325<div class="pdq-content-list">

4326<ul id="_AboutThis_6">

4327<li>

4328be discussed at a meeting,</li>

4329<li>

4330be cited with text, or</li>

4331<li>

4332replace or update an existing article that is already cited.</li>

4333</ul>

4334</div>

4335<p id="_AboutThis_7" tabindex="-1">

4336Changes to the summaries are made through a consensus process in which Board members evaluate the strength of the evidence in the published articles and determine how the article should be included in the summary.</p>

4337<p id="" tabindex="-1">

4338The lead reviewers for Oropharyngeal Cancer Treatment are:</p>

4339<div class="pdq-content-list">

4340<ul id="">

4341<li>

4342Andrea Bonetti, MD (Azienda ULSS 9 of the Veneto Region)</li>

4343<li>

4344Ann W. Gramza, MD (Georgetown Lombardi Comprehensive Cancer Center)</li>

4345<li>

4346Monaliben Patel, MD (Advocate Christ Medical Center)</li>

4347<li>

4348Minh Tam Truong, MD (Boston University Medical Center)</li>

4349</ul>

4350</div>

4351<p id="_AboutThis_9" tabindex="-1">

4352Any comments or questions about the summary content should be submitted to Cancer.gov through the NCI website's <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/contact/email-us" title="https://www.cancer.gov/contact/email-us">

4353Email Us</a>

4354. Do not contact the individual Board Members with questions or comments about the summaries. Board members will not respond to individual inquiries.</p>

4355</section>

4356<section id="_AboutThis_10">

4357<h3 id="_AboutThis_10_toc">

4358Levels of Evidence</h3>

4359<p id="_AboutThis_11" tabindex="-1">

4360Some of the reference citations in this summary are accompanied by a level-of-evidence designation. These designations are intended to help readers assess the strength of the evidence supporting the use of specific interventions or approaches. The PDQ Adult Treatment Editorial Board uses a <a href="/publications/pdq/levels-evidence/treatment">

4361formal evidence ranking system</a>

4362 in developing its level-of-evidence designations.</p>

4363</section>

4364<section id="_AboutThis_12">

4365<h3 id="_AboutThis_12_toc">

4366Permission to Use This Summary</h3>

4367<p id="_AboutThis_13" tabindex="-1">

4368PDQ is a registered trademark. Although the content of PDQ documents can be used freely as text, it cannot be identified as an NCI PDQ cancer information summary unless it is presented in its entirety and is regularly updated. However, an author would be permitted to write a sentence such as &#8220;NCI&#8217;s PDQ cancer information summary about breast cancer prevention states the risks succinctly: [include excerpt from the summary].&#8221;</p>

4369<p id="_AboutThis_14" tabindex="-1">

4370The preferred citation for this PDQ summary is:</p>

4371<p id="_AboutThis_15" tabindex="-1">

Available at: <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/types/head-and-neck/hp/adult/oropharyngeal-treatment-pdq" title="https://www.cancer.gov/types/head-and-neck/hp/adult/oropharyngeal-treatment-pdq">

4373https://www.cancer.gov/types/head-and-neck/hp/adult/oropharyngeal-treatment-pdq</a>

More information on insurance coverage is available on Cancer.gov on the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/managing-care" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/managing-care">
